==PAGE:4==
ii 
 
Abstract 
This research investigates the potential benefits of applying lean thinking principles and 
tools to the warehousing/purchasing processes of Veitur, intending to reduce waste and 
improve both processes and transparency in the supply chain. The research topic was 
chosen due to the problems that Veitur has been having, which is that the inventory has 
been growing, and products are being ordered that ultimately end up not being used.  
This research contains both quantitative and qualitative aspects, as quantitative analysis of 
the inventory database was performed in order to highlight the current inventory problem. 
The qualitative analysis, which consisted of semi-structured interviews, was performed in 
order to identify why the problem is present and identify areas that could be improved to 
reduce the problem. 
Recommendations were made based on the analysis and the literature review, the 
recommendations consisted of applying the principles of lean to the current processes, 
where tools of lean thinking were used in the application of those principles. 
These recommendations yielded positive results, which suggest that applying the principles 
and tools of lean thinking to the warehousing/purchasing processes of Veitur is likely to 
reduce waste in the process and improve both the current processes and transparency 
between different individuals in the supply chain. 
Keywords: Lean thinking, supply chain, process improvement, waste reduction, 
warehousing, purchasing

Introduction

1 Introduction 
1.1 Background 
1.1.1 Warehousing and purchasing processes 
Gwynne Richards (2018) mentions that the role of a supply chain is to deliver the right 
products, in the right quantity, to the right customer, at the right place, at the right time, in 
the right condition, at the right price. A new role that has been added is to deliver at the 
right cost environmentally (Richards, 2014). Purchasing is often associated with doing “the 
five rights”. Getting the right quality of material, at the right time, in the right quantity, for 
the right price, from the right source (Monczka, Handfield, Giunipero, & Patterson, 2016). 
Any company’s warehouse management is an important link in the supply chain since it 
plays a role in the supply chain and the warehouse plays a big role in delivering the right 
products in the right quantity. The warehouse also makes sure that the correct products get 
to the right customer at the right time by making sure that every item is labelled correctly, 
accessible, and loaded onto the correct vehicle. The warehouse then needs to make sure that 
the product leaves the warehouse clean and in good condition (Richards, 2014). Purchasing 
processes can benefit from the addition of lean principles, most companies focus on only 
reducing material cost as opposed to utilizing the philosophy of lean and continuous 
improvement in their purchasing processes, which can help identify non-traditional sources 
of waste and bring benefits to the entire supply chain (Myerson, 2019).  
A way to improve the warehousing and purchasing processes of a company is to use the 
principles and tools of lean thinking (Richards, 2014) (Myerson, 2019). They were defined 
by Womack and Jones (2003) as Value, the value stream, flow, pull and perfection. The 
core of these principles is identifying the value that the customer wants and drawing up all 
the processes that are needed to give the customer that value. Then the organization wants 
to improve the flow of products through their process by eliminating activities that do not 
add value and having the customer pull the product through the process as opposed to 
ordering or manufacturing in batches when there is no need. Lastly, the organization should 
do these steps over and over again and strive towards continuous improvement because 
there are always ways to make the process more efficient. Companies that implement these 
5 principles in their warehousing/purchasing process can see an increase in their customer 
==PAGE:13==
 
Introduction 
Master Thesis 
2 
 
satisfaction and an improvement in their bottom line. By utilizing the principles of lean 
thinking, an organization can gain a variety of benefits, such as a decrease in lead times, 
inventory, unnecessary paperwork, and costs while increasing customer satisfaction at the 
same time (Womack & Jones, 2003).  
1.1.2 Background of “Veitur Utilities ohf” 
The case company for this project has been having problems related to their 
warehousing/purchasing processes which has led to it having a large inventory of products 
that is expensive to keep and a lot of inventory that is deteriorating and will likely never be 
used. 
Reykjavík Energy is a power and utility company that provides households, companies and 
institutions in around the capital area of Iceland with natural resources sustainably and cost-
effectively through their four subsidiaries (Reykjavík Energy, OR Function, n.d.). 
Veitur Utilities ohf, usually just called “Veitur” is a part of the collection of energy 
companies that operate as subsidiaries of the parent company called Reykjavík Energy. The 
other companies in the group of subsidiaries are ON Power, Reykjavík Fibre Network and 
Carbfix (Reykjavík Energy, OR Subsidiaries, n.d.). Reykjavík Energy manages the 
inventory for all its subsidiary companies and manages all purchases that go through them. 
Veitur pays 7,3% yearly interest every month on everything that they choose to keep in the 
warehouse and 17% markup for every product that they check out from the warehouse 
(Veitur, tableau.or.is). 
Veitur Utilities is the biggest utility company in Iceland and they make sure that the 
households of Iceland have access to both hot and cold water, electricity and wastewater 
systems through their distribution systems which are made up of cables and pipes that are 
9000 km in length (Veitur, Veitur Service area, n.d.). Veitur gets its cold water from 
various natural water sources, and they put a lot of effort into preserving the water sources 
so they do not become contaminated and they can continue to offer clean water. Out of all 
the subsidiary companies, Veitur works mostly with ON Power. Together with ON Power 
in the way that they manage the distribution of the hot water and electricity that ON Power 
produces. They distribute the hot water to the greater Reykjavík area as well as some small 
and remote areas, and they distribute the electricity to about half of the population.  Lastly, 
Veitur oversees the collection of wastewater from roughly half of the population which 
==PAGE:14==
 
Introduction 
Master Thesis 
3 
 
they treat at their biological sewage treatment plants and later release into the ocean 
(Veitur, Veitur Operations, n.d.). The area that Veitur services can be seen in Figure 1.1. 
 
Figure 1.1: Veitur's service area (Veitur, Veitur Service area, n.d.) 
1.2 Motivation for research 
The motivation behind this project is the analysis of the warehousing/purchasing process of 
an actual company, finding out what the main contributors to the wastes are, finding out 
what the biggest causes of the growing inventory are, coming up with a plan to reduce the 
weaknesses and waste in the warehousing/purchasing process and streamline the 
warehousing/purchasing process. The author of this project has a great interest in lean 
thinking methodology and supply chains, so it is a great experience to be able to take on a 
real challenge that a company is facing. 
The warehousing/purchasing process at Veitur is far from perfect. Currently, the inventory 
in the warehouse is valued at about 16 million dollars, that is a lot of tied-up capital that 
could be utilized in a better way. There are many different causes for the inventory being 
this high. Some of those reasons include projects that have been cancelled by the city after 
an order has been placed for products specially made for that project and it has either been 
too late to cancel the order, or it was simply forgotten. Then there is the issue with the list 
==PAGE:15==
 
Introduction 
Master Thesis 
4 
 
of available products not being available to everyone that takes part in products going to the 
warehouse, meaning that the transparency is not good enough. Sometimes pipes with a 
specific angle are needed but pipes with the wrong angle get purchased. The parent 
company of Veitur handles the purchasing and they may order something that is available 
at the warehouse since they order products that are asked for by Veitur employees, who do 
not have complete transparency over the inventory. This leads to the accumulation of 
inventory and all these products have a carbon footprint related to producing them and it 
would help to identify ways to reduce that carbon footprint by reducing the need to 
purchase products, by instead using what is available in storage when available. 
(Operations and Innovation leader at Veitur, personal communication, March 15, 2023).  
As can be seen, there are a lot of areas in the supply chain that can be improved, but in this 
project, the focus will be on the warehousing/purchasing process. Veitur needs to improve 
its warehousing/purchasing processes and the transparency between the individuals in the 
supply chain in order to combat the growing inventory situation. The 
warehousing/purchasing process will be defined as the point in the process when the 
creation of the bill of material starts until the product leaves the warehouse.  
Therefore, it is important to compare how the warehousing/purchasing process at Veitur is 
currently to how it could be improved, and the outcome of this project aims to help them by 
identifying what the problems are and act as a route map to the improved 
warehousing/purchasing process, improved transparency and a reduction of inventory.  
The purpose of this mixed method study is to analyze and discover ways to improve the 
warehousing/purchasing processes and reduce waste of the company Veitur Utilities in 
Reykjavík, Iceland. 
1.3 Research question, project objectives, and scope 
Based on the problems that Veitur is having with its current operations, a research question 
has been formulated to pinpoint the focus and objective of the project which can be seen 
below and will be answered in Chapter 7: Conclusion. 
Can the application of lean thinking principles and tools improve the 
warehousing/purchasing processes and reduce waste of the company Veitur Utilities in 
Reykjavík, Iceland? 
==PAGE:16==
 
Introduction 
Master Thesis 
5 
 
In order to answer the research question, the following sub-questions were formed: 
SQ 1: What are the current challenges in Veitur’s warehousing/purchasing processes? 
SQ 2: What is the waste in the process? 
SQ 3: What is the current status of the inventory? 
SQ 4: What is the knowledge of lean thinking for the individuals in the 
warehousing/purchasing process? 
SQ 5: How can the lean thinking principles be applied to improve Veitur’s 
warehousing/purchasing processes? 
In order to guide the process of the research, the following project objectives were 
formulated: 
PO 1: Analyze the inventory at their warehouse and find ways to reduce it. 
PO 2: Analyze and map the current warehousing/purchasing processes. 
PO 3: Identify and eliminate the waste in the warehousing/purchasing process.  
PO 4: Improve transparency between different actors in the supply chain. 
PO 5: Develop a plan to help Veitur improve its warehousing/purchasing processes. 
The scope of this study is limited to Veitur, which is located in Iceland, and companies in 
the supply chain, but the research and application of lean thinking to 
warehousing/purchasing processes can be used as a basis for further research relating to 
different organizations.  
1.4 Learning objectives 
Outlining clear learning objectives is essential. By finishing this master's thesis, the student 
should have developed the capability to: 
• Conduct a well-structured research project that encompasses a research question, 
literature review, methodology, analysis of results, and discussion. 
• Undertake a comprehensive evaluation of the operational processes within the case 
company. 
• Utilize the methodology of lean thinking to identify and eliminate waste within the 
warehousing/purchasing process. 
• Calculate the carbon footprint related to the inventory and find ways to reduce it. 
==PAGE:17==

• Conduct a mixed method research by administering interviews with the managers 
and employees in the supply chain, analyze quantitative inventory data, and present 
the results in an effective manner. 
• Craft a strategy that facilitates the transformation of a company from its current 
processes to improved processes. 
• Present results clearly and concisely. 
• Effectively communicate and present research findings, both in writing and orally. 
1.5 Outline of the thesis 
Chapter 1: Introduction provided background on topics that relate to the research as well as 
the case company, the motivation for conducting the research, the research questions and 
project objectives, as well as the scope and learning objectives. Chapter 2: Literature 
review introduces lean thinking and its origin. The chapter explains the 5 principles of lean 
thinking, the waste in lean thinking, and the lean thinking tools that will be used in the 
research. Chapter 3: Methodology explains the research design as well as both how data 
was collected, and how it was analyzed. This chapter also covers reliability, validation, and 
ethical considerations. Chapter 4: Findings and analysis presents the findings of the 
quantitative analysis and analyses of the qualitative interviews that were performed. 
Chapter 5: Recommendations: Application of lean thinking present recommendations for 
the case company on how the principles and tools of lean thinking could be applied to its 
processes based on the qualitative interviews and the literature review, and how it could 
benefit the case company. Chapter 6: Discussion will discuss the project objectives, where 
they were completed in the recommendation chapter, and some of the key takeaways from 
the project objectives. This chapter then mentions the limitations of the research. Chapter 
7: Conclusion answers the research question and all the sub-questions that were formed. 
This chapter then highlights the key findings of the research. 
 
 
==PAGE:18==
 
Literature review 
Master Thesis 
7 
 
2 Literature review  
Lean thinking is a philosophy and a set of principles with the goal of optimizing processes 
in organizations, with an emphasis on reducing waste, improving efficiency, and delivering 
value to customers. Essentially, lean thinking identifies the value that an organization has to 
offer, increases the volume of value-adding activities and lines up the value-adding 
activities in the most effective sequence. These activities are then conducted when needed, 
without any interruptions to get the efficiency of the process as high as possible. In lean 
thinking, a large focus is allocated to reducing waste in all of the organization's processes. 
Waste is often called “Muda” in the lean philosophy and is defined as an activity that does 
not add value to the customer or the organization (Womack & Jones, 2003). 
Although lean thinking was originally just used by manufacturing companies, it has later 
found success in a variety of sectors. Lean thinking can be applied to all parts of an 
organization’s supply chain and there are many documented examples of lean thinking 
having great success in business processes (Melton, 2005). When applied to the 
warehousing/purchasing process, it can improve workflows, performance, transparency, 
and value-adding activities while reducing time and waste (Richards, 2014) (Myerson, 
2019). NANMO is a Norwegian company that has been a part of a research project called 
Lean Operations and has applied lean principles in its purchasing work. This project is still 
ongoing, but the company has implemented principles of lean, and now the personnel has a 
much clearer description of their responsibility and less overlap between activities, they 
have also educated their personnel on the core activities and can advertise required skills 
more clearly in jobs advertisements, thus improving their value stream and eliminating 
waste. Then cross-functional teams have been investigated in order to increase the 
knowledge and transparency between departments and concentrate better on value-adding 
activities while reducing waste (Torvatn, Pedersen, & Holmen, 2017).  
2.1 Origin of lean thinking 
Lean thinking originated from the Toyota production system (TPS) that was created in 
Japan. TPS was created as a way to improve efficiency and reduce waste in the 
manufacturing process by engineers at Toyota, including Taiichi Ohno and Shigeo Shingo. 
After the conflicts of the second world war, Toyota figured out that the conventual mass 
==PAGE:19==
 
Literature review 
Master Thesis 
8 
 
production, that had been made popular by companies such as Ford Motors was not 
resulting in as much profit as before. Mass production has the focus on reducing costs by 
mass producing a small variety of products, but Toyota wanted to reduce the costs while 
producing a variety of cars in small quantities (Ohno, 1988). A great focus was set on 
continuously improving the processes within the company, setting a long-term vision for 
the company, finding the root causes of problems engaging in continuous innovation, and 
then summarizing the Toyota production system into 14 principles that other companies can 
follow (Liker, 2004). 
This new strategy at Toyota attracted the attention of academics and John Krafcik (1988) 
was the first person to coin the use of the word „Lean“, he did so in his article „Triumph of 
the lean production“ which he wrote while studying at MIT (Krafcik, 1988). The term was 
later defined by James Womack and Daniel Jones (2003) as a way to achieve more by using 
less manpower, less equipment, less time, and less space and at the same time get closer to 
giving their customers exactly what they want (Womack & Jones, 2003).  
Lean thinking has come a long way from its beginnings as a methodology used in repetitive 
assembly line manufacturing. In recent years, it has been applied to supply chain, 
administrative, and operations management functions in all kinds of goods and service 
industries (Myerson, 2019). 
2.2 The principles of lean thinking 
Womack and Jones (2003) defined the 5 principles of lean thinking that act as guidelines 
for optimizing the flow of value to the end customer while minimizing waste (Womack & 
Jones, 2003). These principles have been widely adopted by organizations for all parts of 
the supply chain and they have been shown to be effective in improving operational 
efficiency and customer satisfaction (Melton, 2005) (Myerson, 2019) (Torvatn, Pedersen, & 
Holmen, 2017). The 5 principles of lean thinking as defined by Womack and Jones (2003) 
are the following: 
1. Value 
2. The Value stream 
3. Flow 
4. Pull 
==PAGE:20==
 
Literature review 
Master Thesis 
9 
 
5. Perfection 
These principles can be seen visually in Figure 2.1 and will be explained better in the 
following subchapters by their original definitions and how Torvatn, Pedersen & Holmen 
(2017) defined them for purchasing processes. 
 
Figure 2.1: The 5 principles of lean thinking (Lean Enterprise Institute, n.d.) 
2.2.1 Value 
Value is the first principle of lean thinking and the goal is to specify the value from the 
customer’s standpoint. This means that an organization should understand the specific 
needs of its customers and develop their products and services to satisfy those requirements 
as effectively and efficiently as possible. (Womack & Jones, 2003). For the purchasing 
process, the purchasing personnel needs to be in contact with the customers to understand 
their needs and gain valuable insight into the customers and their concerns. Cross-function 
teams can be beneficial in channeling the customer’s wishes and concerns to other 
departments within the organization and the purchasing personnel should be in good 
contact with the production team to gain an understanding of the material requirements for 
each project (Torvatn, Pedersen, & Holmen, 2017). 
2.2.2 The value stream 
The value stream is the second principle of lean thinking and here it is sought to define all 
the activities in the production process, whether the process produces a product or a service, 
and identify which activities in the process are adding value to the customer. There are 3 
types of activities in the value stream, activities that add value to the customer, activities 
that do not add value to the customer but are necessary, and activities that do not add value 
to the customer and are not necessary. activities that do not add value to the customers are 
==PAGE:21==
 
Literature review 
Master Thesis 
10 
 
classified as waste and the aim here is to reduce or remove those activities to increase the 
efficiency of the value stream, increase customer satisfaction, and reduce costs (Womack & 
Jones, 2003). Similar to other departments, the purchasing department should identify its 
main processes, and break them down into separate activities, this will allow the managers 
to clearly see and analyze all the activities and decide on how they can eliminate activities 
that do not add value while focusing on those that do add value (Torvatn, Pedersen, & 
Holmen, 2017).  
2.2.3 Flow 
Flow is the third principle of lean thinking. The aim here is to let products or information 
flow continuously through the value-adding activities in the process. The principle of flow 
is based on a concept that was used by Toyota and is called just-in-time (JIT). JIT is a 
method that is used to achieve flow production by sending parts in the right quantity at the 
right time. In flow production, a product is allowed to flow through the production process 
without interruptions or waiting times. Flow production can be seen as the opposite of 
traditional batch production where products have to wait between stages until the whole 
batch has been completed. Batch production is widely used because it seems to be efficient 
in maximizing the utilization of machines and personnel. However, in flow production, it is 
important to focus on the part that is travelling through the process instead of focusing on 
the resources that operate on the part. The principle achieves this by improving the value 
stream by reducing setup times, reducing changeover times, and improving quality control. 
By doing this, an organization can make their process be able to respond quickly to changes 
in customer demand and increase the efficiency of the process (Womack & Jones, 2003). 
For a support function, like purchasing, one of the ways to improve the flow is to avoid 
waste, that is putting the most focus on value-adding activities while eliminating or 
reducing the non-value-adding activities. The purchasing department should try to reduce 
the waste of time and resources in storage and try to coordinate storage and deliveries to 
achieve a stream of materials that enter the supply chain as close to JIT as possible. They 
need to be up to date on project plans and schedules to make sure to have the right amount 
of material available. The purchasing department should make sure that the purchasing and 
payment processes are simple and standardized to eliminate the waste that could follow 
mistakes in that process (Torvatn, Pedersen, & Holmen, 2017). 
==PAGE:22==
 
Literature review 
Master Thesis 
11 
 
2.2.4 Pull 
Pull is the fourth principle of lean thinking. The aim here is to introduce a pull between all 
the activities where a constant flow is a possibility. The principle of pull refers to the fact 
that the customer “pulls” the product and nothing is produced until the customer places an 
order. The customers can be external or other departments within the organization. The 
main focus here is to not produce anything until the customer needs it, and when the 
customer finally needs it, the production should be fast. That is made possible by reducing 
throughput time with the introduction of flow production. When pull has been implemented 
in the process, demand schedules should not be needed anymore. The principle of pull and 
a short throughput time allows organizations to deliver a product at a reasonable time 
without having to keep excess inventory that can end up unsold (Womack & Jones, 2003). 
Purchasing departments typically operate based on derived demand (van Weele, 1994), and 
it can come from a customer order or from “internal” customers. The purchasing 
department is the middleman to external suppliers and can experience a mismatch between 
internal order time and external delivery times. It can be tried to negotiate with suppliers to 
help them reduce their lead times or establish good communication with suppliers and 
improve information systems between companies (Torvatn, Pedersen, & Holmen, 2017). 
2.2.5 Perfection 
Perfection is the fifth principle of lean thinking. When an organization has completed the 
first 4 principles, they realize quickly that all those previous principles can be improved 
even more. It is a cycle that never stops, getting value to flow faster through the value 
stream always exposes hidden waste in the value stream that can be tackled and as the pull 
gets stronger, more obstacles to the pull are found that can be removed. In short, the 
perfection principle focuses on continuous improvement of the organizational process 
(Womack & Jones, 2003). In the purchasing process, it means that people in the process, 
processes, and systems should improve continuously. Measures should be developed that 
keep track of the performances of activities in the process, and they should be tracked over 
time. The results of the measures should be shared with all parties that are involved 
(Torvatn, Pedersen, & Holmen, 2017). 
==PAGE:23==
 
Literature review 
Master Thesis 
12 
 
2.3 Waste “Muda”  
The concept of waste reduction is one of the main focus of lean thinking. Therefore, it is 
important to know what the wastes are to be able to identify them efficiently within 
organizations. Taiichi Ohno (1988), an industrial engineer at Toyota is credited with being 
the first to emphasize waste reduction within an organization. Ohno wanted to focus on 
reducing waste, or as he called it “Muda” which means waste in Japanese. Ohno identified 
seven types of waste that could be eliminated to reduce cost and increase efficiency and 
later, others added an eighth waste which will be used in this project. The word “Muda” 
does not sound good and that is the point, since it represents waste. Waste is classified as a 
human activity that utilizes resources without creating any value at all. This can mean a 
defect in a product that calls for a correction, or production of items when there is no need 
for them so they will end up as unused inventory, unnecessary operations, and unnecessary 
movement of personnel and products. A bottleneck is also classified as a waste where a 
group of workers cannot start their work until another group of workers upstream finishes 
their work (Womack & Jones, 2003). 
The eight wastes can be seen in Figure 2.2 and they will be described in the following 
subchapters. 
 
Figure 2.2: The eight wastes (Myerson, 2019) 
==PAGE:24==
 
Literature review 
Master Thesis 
13 
 
2.3.1 Overproduction 
Overproduction is a waste where an organization decides to produce in excess to have stock 
ready for emergencies and get ahead of the production schedule. This is not only a waste 
due to the work it takes to create the extra product, but it also leads to another type of waste 
that is classified as having excess inventory (Imai, 2012). Overproduction can also be 
linked to ordering, excessive ordering without an ordering request can lead to extra 
unnecessary paperwork and having more work-in-process or finished goods inventory 
(Myerson, 2019). 
2.3.2 Waiting 
The waste of waiting can refer to personnel, materials, and systems. When an operator at a 
station is not doing anything due to having to wait for material, if the machine is 
experiencing downtime, or if the worker is observing the machine while it works it is 
considered a waste (Imai, 2012). Furthermore, waiting can be related to employees in the 
supply chain waiting for data, approvals for projects, material from suppliers, or the 
processing of purchase orders and contracts (Myerson, 2019). 
2.3.3 Transporting 
Transport is a necessary part of organizational operations, but they should however be kept 
to a minimum since a lot of the transportation does not add any value. Transportation does 
not only mean moving a product from one place to another, but it is also about the 
transportation of information in the form of email chains and paperwork. While a product is 
being transported, it is not gaining any value and could even get destroyed in the process. It 
is therefore helpful to locate production processes close to one another and get information 
across quickly and efficiently to minimize this type of waste (Imai, 2012) (Myerson, 2019). 
2.3.4 Overprocessing 
Overprocessing is defined as doing more work on a product or a service than is necessary to 
meet the needs of the customer. Often, there are a lot of processes in the value stream that 
do not add value or could be performed in a more efficient and faster way (Imai, 2012). It 
can also be in the form of processing a purchase order and order acknowledgements 
(Myerson, 2019). 
==PAGE:25==
 
Literature review 
Master Thesis 
14 
 
2.3.5 Inventory 
Inventory is one of the most common and largest sources of waste in manufacturing and 
supply chain operations. All types of products, whether it is complete products, product 
parts, or supplies that are kept in inventory do not add any value. They only increase costs 
since need to be stored somewhere, sorted, and rely on personnel working for operation and 
administration. The quality of the products deteriorates over time and can even get 
destroyed in the case of an earthquake or a fire. Organizations usually generate inventory 
by producing or ordering in excess due to poor organizational transparency or inaccurate 
information in material requirement plans (Imai, 2012) (Myerson, 2019). 
2.3.6 Motion 
This applies both to people and machinery, all unnecessary motion is considered a waste. 
Walking, bending, searching, or sending documents back and forth does not add any value, 
so it is important to have everything organized and all material or documents located in a 
place that is near to the place it is needed (Imai, 2012) (Myerson, 2019).  
2.3.7 Defects 
Defects in products or paperwork are an expensive and time-consuming problem that can 
happen in many areas of the organizational process. Often defects require a product to be 
remade from scratch or a rework, which can happen due to faulty machinery, or it can be 
that the information about material requirements was not efficiently received, processed, or 
not up to standards. It is therefore important to standardize the material used and the forms 
used to request them and make them as understandable as possible to prevent defects due to 
poor paperwork (Imai, 2012) (Myerson, 2019). 
2.3.8 Skills 
The waste of skill happens when employees spend too much of their time doing 
administrative tasks rather than applying their knowledge and skills to work that 
contributes to continuous improvement within the organization, such as improving the 
collaboration between internal and external supply chain partners. This can be due to long 
lead times, incorrect material schedules, and unsynchronized processes (Myerson, 2019).  
==PAGE:26==
 
Literature review 
Master Thesis 
15 
 
2.4 Lean thinking tools and techniques  
Lean thinking uses a variety of different tools in order to eliminate waste and increase 
efficiency (Womack & Jones, 2003). In this chapter, the researcher will describe some of 
the tools that are often used in lean thinking.  
2.4.1 5S 
5S is a tool that focuses on eliminating waste withing an organization by utilizing 5 
different steps. The name originates from the first letter of the 5 steps in Japanese, Seiri, 
Seiton, Seiso, Seiketsu, and Shitsuke which translates to sort, straighten, shine, standardize, 
and sustain. By involving 5S in the workplace, an organization can expect an increase in 
efficiency and workplace safety while reducing waste. Organizations can use this tool in 
order to continuously improve their operations (Imai, 2012) (Liker, 2004). 5S is identified 
as one of the most promising methods of improving an existing system, there are examples 
of 5S reducing waste, reducing time searching for items, improving utilization of 
workspace, reducing time in completing a task, reducing defects, and improving 
productivity (Bharambe, Patel, Moradiya, & Acharaya, 2020). In Figure 2.3, the 5 steps of 
5S can be seen and they will be described in the next subchapters.  
 
Figure 2.3: The 5 steps of 5S (Liker, 2004) 
2.4.1.1 Sort “Seiri” 
Sort is the first step in 5S and it entails classifying all items in the workspace into two 
categories, necessary items, and unnecessary items, and eventually removing the 
unnecessary items from the workspace. A good rule of thumb is to remove every item that 
==PAGE:27==
 
Literature review 
Master Thesis 
16 
 
is not likely to be used in the next 30 days. A good way to identify just how many items 
might be unnecessary is to place a red tag on all the items that are believed to be 
unnecessary and see just how many red tags will be placed. By removing all the 
unnecessary items from the workspace, the workers will have a safer and cleaner place to 
work which gives them more flexibility in their work area and keep it more organized 
(Imai, 2012). This can also include outdated and damaged goods, over stock, broken 
equipment, and more (Richards, 2014).  
2.4.1.2 Straighten “Seiton” 
After all the items have been sorted, it is important to find a designated place for all the 
necessary items because these items are useless if they cannot be found when needed. The 
designated places for every item should be in a space close to where it will be needed and 
every employee should learn where every item is kept so that it will be accessible quickly 
and returned to its designated place after use (Imai, 2012). 
2.4.1.3 Shine “Seiso” 
This step involves keeping everything in the workspace clean. When everything is clean, it 
becomes much easier to identify something that could become a big problem in the future if 
nothing is changed (Imai, 2012).  
2.4.1.4 Standardize “Seiketsu” 
This step highlights the importance of doing the previous 3 steps regularly. If the 3 steps 
are only conducted once and even though the workspace is clean and organized, it will 
likely return to its previous stage. It is therefore important to schedule them regularly to 
strive towards continuous improvement and that is why it is important to have the support 
of managers who decide how often these steps will be done (Imai, 2012). Here it can also 
refer to standardized ways to perform so that everyone does them in the same documented 
manner (Myerson, 2019) 
2.4.1.5 Sustain “Shitsuke” 
Shitsuke means self-discipline and it refers to the discipline that is needed to sustain a clean 
environment at the workstation. It should eventually become a habit for everyone in the 
organization to keep their workspace clean in order to be able to identify abnormalities that 
could become a problem. Managers should have set standards for each of the previous steps 
that should include ways to evaluate the progress at each step (Imai, 2012). 
==PAGE:28==
 
Literature review 
Master Thesis 
17 
 
2.4.2 The 5 why method (Root cause analysis) 
The 5 why’s is a simple but effective problem-solving technique that is regularly used 
along lean thinking and continuous improvement processes. The technique was developed 
by Sakichi Toyoda and later described by Taiichi Ohno, the architect behind the Toyota 
production system (Ohno, 1988).  
The method involves asking involves asking yourself “why” questions in order to get to the 
root cause of a problem and according to Ohno, you can get to the root of any problem by 
continuing to as “why” after every answer until you reach the root cause (Ohno, 1988). 
“The basis of Toyota’s scientific approach is to ask why five times whenever we find a 
problem … By repeating why five times, the nature of the problem as well as its solution 
becomes clear“ (Ohno, 1988, p. 123). 
When a problem has been defined, you should ask yourself, why this problem happens and 
then ask “why“ based on the answer to the previous question. It is important to keep asking 
why until the root of the original problem has been identified and when the root has been 
identified, the next step is to develop and implement measures to prevent the problem from 
happening again (Ohno, 1988). 
Figure 2.4 shows visually how the method works. The method is not limited to asking 
“why” 5 times, but usually that is enough to get to the root of a problem. 
 
Figure 2.4: The 5 whys method (Kanbanize, n.d.) 
Womack and Jones mention that the 5 why’s is an effective tool since it forces individuals 
to really think about a problem and its causes rather than just reacting to it. It requires a 
==PAGE:29==
 
Literature review 
Master Thesis 
18 
 
disciplined approach which usually leads to more sustainable and effective solutions than 
only fixing what is wrong at a given time. The 5 whys method is generally easy to do since 
it is a really simple and cost-effective way to improve processes and solve problems 
(Womack & Jones, 2003). The method does not require any specific tools or equipment and 
can be used to solve almost any problem that arises. This method also incentivizes an 
organizational culture of continuous improvement, where individuals continuously look for 
ways to eliminate waste and improve the efficiency of processes (Ohno, 1988). 
2.4.3 Standardization  
Standard operating procedures (SOP) can be described as methods and rules that have been 
written down that an employee can follow in order to perform a daily business activity. 
Standardization is one of the key elements of lean thinking and an essential part of 
achieving continuous improvement and efficiency within organizations. Masaaki Imai 
(2012) identified the following key features of standards: 
• Standards represent the most efficient, easiest, and safest way to perform a task and 
are a result of wisdom and expertise gained over time by employees in their 
respected roles. 
• Standards provide the best way to retain knowledge and expertise in the case of an 
employee leaving the organization. 
• Standards offer a means of evaluating performance. 
• Standards demonstrate the relationship between both cause and effect. A lack of 
standards or a failure to adhere to standards always results in discrepancies, 
variation, and waste. 
• Standards serve as a foundation for both maintenance and improvement. Following 
standards indicates maintenance, whereas upgrading standards indicates 
improvement. 
2.4.4 Digital visual management 
Visual management is an important concept in lean thinking, it utilizes visual aids, such as 
signs, graphs, charts, and more in order to share information about the status of a project, 
the progress of processes, and any problem that comes up (Womack & Jones, 2003). This 
improves the understanding of the process as a whole for employees and allows them to 
make decisions more efficiently. By increasing the visibility of problems and errors, it is 
==PAGE:30==
 
Literature review 
Master Thesis 
19 
 
likely that a culture of continuous improvement forms since employees can quickly see 
when things go wrong and can identify areas in the process that can be improved to prevent 
the same errors from appearing again (Imai, 2012). Visual management can also help in 
standardizing organizational processes by using visual aids to provide clear guidelines that 
employees can follow to increase their efficiency, reduce mistakes due to human errors, and 
ensure that a process is completed consistently (Womack & Jones, 2003). With technology 
advancing, methods to use visual management with the help of technology have been 
identified, resulting in the term digital visual management (DVM). Digital interactive 
interfaces can be used as a digital dashboard to display information to those who need it 
when needed, digital andon devices, such as smartphones or computers can be utilized to 
trigger notifications for the employees, and Poka-yoke can be used to prevent human errors 
with the help of modern technology (Romero, Zanchi, Powell, & Gaiardelli, 2022) (Mayr, 
et al., 2018).  
2.4.5 Poka-yoke 
Poka-yoke is a system that Shigeo Shingo, an engineer who contributed to the Toyota 
manufacturing system, is credited for creating. Poka yoke is just the Japanese term for 
“mistake-proofing” and it aims at eliminating human errors that can lead to defects or 
makes the error obvious so that it can be corrected. There are 2 different methods of Poka-
yoke systems, that is control methods and warning methods. The control method does not 
allow a process to continue if abnormalities are detected. The warning method calls the 
employee's attention in the case of abnormalities that could result in defects, the employee 
would then need to respond to the situation (Shingo, 1986). Advanced technology, like 
auto-identification systems, can ensure accurate documentation of items and prevent 
incorrect documentation due to human error (Mayr, et al., 2018).   
2.4.6 Andon 
Andon was one of the bases for quality in the Toyota Production System and is a visual 
management tool. Andon is the process of employees pushing a button or pulling a chord in 
the case of abnormalities, which results in a visual alert, notifying the one responsible so 
that countermeasures can be taken as soon as possible (Liker, 2004). The alerts are often 
color-coded, with different colors having different meanings (Reyes, Morales, Aldas, 
Reyes, & Toasa, 2019). As technology has become more widely used in processes, the use 
==PAGE:31==
 
Literature review 
Master Thesis 
20 
 
of targeted andon signals being sent by computers of smartphones in real-time regardless of 
the distance between the employes has gotten more common (Mayr, et al., 2018). Reyes et 
al. (2019) identified that the implementation of a digital andon system in a shoe production 
environment reduced response time to defects. 
2.4.7 Kaizen 
Continuous improvement is often referred to as “kaizen” and kaizen is just the Japanese 
word for continuous improvement and according to Masaaki Imai (2012), one of the tools 
to ensure continuous improvement is “Plan-Do-Check-Act” (PDCA). Organizations do this 
cycle, again and again, to identify areas that need improvement and come up with solutions 
to them that will be standardized (Imai, 2012). The cycle can be seen in Figure 2.5 and the 
individual steps will be described according to Masaaki Imai (2012) below. 
 
Figure 2.5: The Plan-Do-Check-Act circle (Imai, 2012) 
Plan: Plan refers to identifying a target that could be improved and creating a plan to 
achieve those improvements. 
Do: Do refers to implementing the plan that was created in the previous step. 
Check: Check refers to analyzing the results of the changes that were made in the previous 
step to see if they were successful or not.  
Act: Act refers to taking action based on the results found in the previous step. If the goals 
were met, then it may be important to standardize the changes made in the process and if 
==PAGE:32==
 
Literature review 
Master Thesis 
21 
 
the goals were not met, then the organization needs to start again in the PDCA cycle and 
come up with a new plan to achieve their goals. 
2.4.8 Supplier relationship management?  
Supplier relationship management (SRM) is a method that aims to streamline the processes 
between an organization and its suppliers and make them more effective (Momiwand & 
Shahin, 2012). SRM does not have a set organizational structure and can range from 
individual relationships to organizational relationships (Myerson, 2019). Momiwand & 
Shahin (2012) suggested mapping the suppliers by identifying the most important products 
and the most important suppliers, and grouping them as can be seen in Table 2.1.   
Table 2.1: Supplier mapping (Momiwand & Shahin, 2012) 
  
Low-volume purchases 
High-volume purchases 
Critical product suppliers 
Bottleneck suppliers 
Strategic suppliers 
Non-critical product suppliers 
Non-critical suppliers 
Leverage suppliers 
For non-critical suppliers, the organization should keep an arm’s length relationship, for 
leverage suppliers, it is suggested to keep a mid-term relationship. For bottleneck suppliers 
and strategic suppliers, it is recommended that long-term relationships should be formed. 
After identifying the most important suppliers, the organization should first look inwards 
and find ways to improve its own processes. After that, the organization can build a 
relationship with the selected suppliers. Key projects should then be selected by 
importance, and the organization should integrate their objectives and performances with 
the suppliers. The details of the implementation should be made visible, and the 
organization should provide new angles to the existing knowledge that the suppliers 
possess and provide the education and resources necessary to achieve the expectations. The 
implementation should be monitored and measured using performance metrics and make 
adjustments if necessary. (Momiwand & Shahin, 2012). Pompaj Co. is a company that 
improved their supplier relationships with the aim of reducing lead times, they managed to 
reduce the procurement time (average time from ordering, until receiving the product) from 
5-8 days to 1-3 days. Customer time (the time from manufacturing, until it reaches the 
customer) reduced from between 10-15 days to 5-10 days (Momiwand & Shahin, 2012).  
==PAGE:33==
 
Literature review 
Master Thesis 
22 
 
2.4.9 Key performance indicators 
Key performance indicators (KPIs) are quantifiable measures that an organization can use 
to measure its performance over time in order to determine an organization’s progress in 
achieving its goals. Effective KPIs quantify waste and offer early warnings for processes 
that are operating outside of the SOP (Helmold, 2020).

Methodology 
Master Thesis

3 Methodology 
This section outlines the methods used to perform the research and collect and analyze data 
in order to answer the research question and complete the project objectives. The section 
provides an overview of the research design, data collection, and analysis used in this 
research, as well as the reliability, validity, and ethical considerations. This research project 
revolves around the research question:  
Can the application of lean thinking principles and tools improve the 
warehousing/purchasing processes and reduce waste of the company Veitur Utilities in 
Reykjavík, Iceland? 
The following sub-questions: 
SQ 1: What are the current challenges in Veitur’s warehousing/purchasing processes? 
SQ 2: What is the waste in the process? 
SQ 3: What is the current status of the inventory? 
SQ 4: What is the knowledge of lean thinking for the individuals in the 
warehousing/purchasing process? 
SQ 5: How can the lean thinking principles be applied to improve Veitur’s 
warehousing/purchasing processes? 
And the following project objectives: 
PO 1: Analyze the inventory at their warehouse and find ways to reduce it. 
PO 2: Analyze and map the current warehousing/purchasing processes. 
PO 3: Identify and eliminate the waste in the warehousing/purchasing process.  
PO 4: Improve transparency between different actors in the supply chain. 
PO 5: Develop a plan to help Veitur improve its warehousing/purchasing processes. 
3.1 Research design 
The research was originally thought to be qualitative research but the researcher soon 
realized that he would also use quantitative methods. As Bryman highlighted in his 2006 
study, some arguments support the use of mixed research as can be seen below (Bryman, 
2006): 
==PAGE:35==
 
Methodology 
Master Thesis 
24 
 
1. Triangulation: researchers analyze both the quantitative and qualitative data and 
triangulate the findings to corroborate the results of the different methods. 
2. Complementary: researchers aim to elaborate, enhance, illustrate, or clarify the 
findings of one method with the results from the other. 
3. Development: researchers use the results of one method to assist in developing or 
informing the other method. Here, development can refer to sampling, 
measurements, and measurement decisions. 
4. Initiation: Researchers aim to discover paradoxes and contradictions, identify new 
perspectives, and transform questions or outcomes of one approach by integrating 
them with questions or outcomes of another approach. 
5. Expansion: Researchers aim to expand the scope of enquiry by using different 
methods for the different inquiry components. 
The purpose of the mixed method in this study is to be complementary. The research will 
mainly focus on the qualitative aspect while the quantitative method will serve as a tool in 
highlighting the current problems. The qualitative method used is semi-structured 
interviews that will be conducted with those that have a direct effect on the inventory that 
ends up in the warehouse, and a quantitative analysis will be made on the inventory 
database to show that there is a problem present and identify the main areas contributing to 
the growing inventory. Lastly, the principles and tools of lean thinking will be used in order 
to increase the transparency of the processes that lead to the growing inventory and aim to 
reduce the tied-up capital in the warehouse and reduce the inventory. 
The following is a description of Figure 3.1. Conducting the initial literature review while 
focusing on implementing lean thinking made it clear that it would benefit the research 
greatly by incorporating quantitative methods as well in order to make it clear why things 
need to improve in the warehouse that Veitur uses. The scope of the research was decided 
to be the inventory that Veitur had, but that also involves Reykjavík Energy which handles 
the ordering of products on behalf of Veitur, and the company called Eimskip which 
manages the warehouse that Veitur uses along with the other subsidiary companies. The 
initial themes that were generated from conversations with various employees and the help 
of the literature review helped in creating the semi-structured interviews that were 
conducted. The research methods were both qualitative and quantitative as was explained in 
==PAGE:36==
 
Methodology 
Master Thesis 
25 
 
the last paragraph, this is something that the researcher deemed to be important since just 
doing the interviews, he thinks that the initial problem that is going on would not be as 
clear. That is why the quantitative method was conducted as well.  
 
Figure 3.1: The research design, authors adaption from (Bryman, Sloan, Foster, & Clark, 
2021) 
3.2 Data collection 
This section will cover which methods were used to acquire the data to conduct the 
research, as well as the reasoning for choosing those methods. The research is mostly based 
on qualitative interviews to get an understanding of the current status of Veitur’s processes, 
while a quantitative database analysis is used to highlight the need for a change by looking 
at the company’s inventory management data. The literature review will then be used as a 
==PAGE:37==
 
Methodology 
Master Thesis 
26 
 
basis for the creation of questions for the interview and a way to work with the data 
gathered from both the interviews and database analysis. 
3.2.1.1 Veitur’s warehouse inventory database 
Veitur uses an application called Tableau to compile data from the software Unit4 and plot 
it in order to visualize the data more clearly. In Tableau, it is possible to apply filters for the 
time a product has been in stock, the type of product, and more. An initial investigation of 
the inventory was conducted where the data in Tableau was analyzed with regards to the 3 
product categories that Veitur has, that is routine products, special products, and spare 
parts. The goal was to see the changes in the inventory status over the last couple of years 
and generate ideas of what product category might be the largest contributor to that growth.  
3.2.1.2 Greenhouse gas measures 
Every product that is purchased has a greenhouse gas release related to the production of 
that product. Carbon emissions related to products that are not used can be looked at as a 
waste since requesting a product to be created leads to a release of greenhouse gases 
whether the product will eventually be used or not. Reykjavík Energy uses greenhouse gas 
standards that are based on the ones that were released by DEFRA (Department for 
environment food and rural affairs) (Specialist in climate and environmental issues at 
Reykjavík energy, personal communication, April 28, 2023) which is a British government 
department that is responsible for protecting the environment (DEFRA, n.d.). The 
greenhouse gas standard gives the kg of greenhouse gas that is released for every Great 
British Pound spent on various materials. The greenhouse gas standards used can be seen in 
Appendix B. The researcher will analyze the inventory that will be identified as the most 
problematic from the inventory database research and calculate the carbon emissions 
related to those products. The researcher will also work with the specialist in climate and 
environmental issues at Reykjavík energy to arrange all the products of the product group 
that is the most problematic from the inventory management database research to their 
respected greenhouse gas categories in order to highlight the waste of greenhouse gas 
released, which is something that both Veitur and Reykjavík energy mention that they want 
to reduce (Reykjavík Energy, Sustainable development goals, n.d.) (Reykjavík Energy, E1 
Greenhouse gas emissions, n.d.).  
==PAGE:38==
 
Methodology 
Master Thesis 
27 
 
3.2.1.3 Interviews  
When deciding what type of interviews were going to be conducted, the researcher decided 
to perform semi-structured interviews since those can be beneficial if the researcher has a 
fairly clear focus on what should be analyzed and it allows the researcher to address 
specific issues (Bryman, Sloan, Foster, & Clark, 2021). Before creating the interview, it 
was important to identify the individuals that were going to be interviewed and the 
researcher identified those individuals as those in the supply chain who have a direct effect 
on the inventory that ends up in the warehouse. 
• External consultants (sometimes called designers): The external consultants are 
outsourced to engineering companies and they are those who draw construction 
projects and estimate the bill of materials that will be needed to complete a project. 
Interviewing them will give an insight into their methods, how accurate the bill of 
material usually is, and how their communication and flow of information is to 
other actors in the supply chain.  
• Internal designers: The internal designers are the individuals that receive the bill of 
materials from the external consultants and review it. Interviewing them will give 
an insight into the processes of reviewing the bill of materials, and their 
communication with the external consultants. 
• Project managers: The project managers are individuals at Veitur, and they oversee 
the construction projects that will take place. They are the individuals that make a 
purchasing request with the purchasing department. Interviewing them will give the 
researcher an insight into the processes that they follow before placing a purchase 
order, how information flows between the project managers and other individuals, 
and how they handle projects that are cancelled.  
• Purchasing department: The purchasing department receives the purchasing requests 
and is responsible for purchases to be done. It will be helpful to see how they select 
their suppliers, how their communication is with the warehouse managers, and how 
they utilize the available data to assist them in making purchases. 
• Warehouse managers: The warehouse managers are tasked with making sure that 
products are received, stored properly, and inspected to make sure that they are in 
good condition, and get them ready to be picked up. It will be interesting to see how 
they store the products, how they keep track of the inventory and the transparency 
==PAGE:39==
 
Methodology 
Master Thesis 
28 
 
between them and the purchasing department, and what methods they use in 
receiving and making a product ready to be picked up.  
The semi-structured interviews were created with the initial themes that were chosen as a 
focus for the interviews. The themes are the following:  
• Process improvements: in this theme, the focus was on getting an understanding of 
how current processes are for the department in question. Since the interviews were 
conducted in 5 different places in the supply chain, the questions were not always 
the same. For example, when interviewing the external consultants, the focus was 
on getting to know the processes involved in creating the bill of materials while the 
interviews with the purchasing department focused more on the processes that 
follow the procurement of the product or material in question. Here, it was also 
important to gain an understanding of the standards, software, and computer 
programs that the department in question uses in its processes.  
• Communication: here, it was good to get an understanding of which actors in the 
supply chain the different departments that were asked are in the most contact with, 
how that communication takes place, and what communication problems have 
occurred. It was also important to get an understanding of how the transparency 
between different actors in the supply chain is, especially with regard to the 
accessibility of the inventory computer programs for different departments in the 
supply chain. 
• Waste: Here, it was good to ask the interviewees to think about what type of waste 
is the most problematic in the organization. They were also asked about why 
products end up not being used and stored in the warehouse, how they try to 
prevent that from happening, and why they keep those products for a long time.  
• Implementation: It is good to know if the person in question knows the 
methodology of lean thinking and if they currently are using some of the tools that 
lean thinking has to offer. The interviewees were also asked if they have looked 
into implementing the lean thinking principles or tools in their operations.  
• Environment: Here, it was important to get to know if the departments in question 
are measuring the carbon footprint related to their products, and if they are actively 
trying to reduce it.  
==PAGE:40==
 
Methodology 
Master Thesis 
29 
 
These themes were chosen because, as seen in the literature review, these are areas that can 
benefit from implementing lean thinking. As stated in the literature review, waste reduction 
is one of the biggest parts of lean thinking, and the carbon footprint related to products that 
will not be used is considered a waste. Lean thinking seeks to improve processes and can 
reduce unnecessary communication with increased visibility and transparency. 
The questions were therefore categorized by these themes, derived based on what should be 
important to get knowledge on according to the literature review, and most questions were 
open in order to allow the interviewee to give a detailed answer. The interviews took place 
in a meeting room where the interviewee in question works and they were all face to face. 
All the interviews began with a short introduction about the research and they were 
conducted in Icelandic and recorded and then transcribed in English. A transcription of all 
the interviews can be seen in Appendix C. Table 3.1 shows the details of every interview.
